Chapter 1

CHAPTER 1

Havana’s light was wrong. Not the color, but the weight of it—a thick, honeyed syrup that coated the peeling shutters of this rented room and made every thought a slow swim through warm molasses. New York light had been a blade, sharp and clarifying. Here, in the supposed cradle of memory, everything blurred at the edges.




The cup is empty. The grounds are a black, fractal landscape at the bottom. I tilt it, watch the sludge cling. My father read coffee grounds. In the cramped kitchen smelling of cardamom and fried onions, women would bring him their cups, their voices hushed, urgent. He would peer into the tiny, bitter world, his glasses slipping down his nose, and speak of journeys and letters and shadows near the heart. He called it ‘ilm al-finjān, the science of the cup. A science of maybe. A poetry of stains. I never learned it. I preferred the certainty of grammar, the architecture of a clean sentence. Subject, verb, object. A place for everything. Now my sentences are rubble. My subjects are fugitive.

“Encore?”

The waiter is a greyhound in a black apron. He has asked three times. His pencil hovers over the pad. The economy of patience. I am a bad investment. I have been here two hours, one coffee. I am occupying a four-top, my papers spread like a patient etherized upon a table. No, not my line. His line. Another ghost at the table.

“Un café. S’il vous plaît.”

My French is correct, accent heavy, a stone in the shoe of the language. He nods, whisks the old cup away. The man at the other table folds his newspaper with a crisp, final snap. He leaves coins on the marble, a precise, melodic click. He has a place to be. A function. I watch him go, his silhouette cutting the liar’s light at the door.

The new coffee arrives, a tiny black depth in a white cup. I do not drink it. I light a cigarette instead. Gauloises. The tobacco is harsh, philosophical. It tastes of existentialism and black turtlenecks and conversations I never had. I inhale, let the smoke pool in my lungs, a familiar, burning anchor. My papers are a chaos of false starts. A novel, I told the man at the embassy, the one with the kind, tired eyes who stamped my temporary visa. Je suis écrivain. I said it with a straight face. A writer. What I am is a recorder of silences. A curator of gaps.

The notebook open before me is not for the novel. It is a ledger of absence. Today’s entry: The smell of jasmine in the evening. Not here. There. In the courtyard after the first watering. A specific damp-earth, white-flower scent that arrived with the dusk. It is not a generic jasmine. It is the jasmine that grew by the west wall, where the bricks were cracked from the roots. That jasmine.

I close the book. The ghost of the scent is stronger than the coffee, the cigarette. It is an ache in the nasal memory, a physical twist behind the eyes.

A woman sits at the table by the window, the one recently vacated. She places a leather portfolio carefully on the chair beside her. She is perhaps sixty, well-kept, a cashmere scarf the color of a dove’s throat knotted at her neck. She orders a noisette. Her hands are calm. She does not look at me, but I feel her awareness, the polite, Parisian radar that notes and files. An exile is a suspicious object. We are loose items in a well-ordered room. We have edges that don’t align.

I force myself back to the novel. The one I am supposed to be writing. The story of a man who leaves everything behind. Original. The page is blank except for a single line, written and rewritten: He woke to the sound of traffic, which was not the sound he expected.

It’s no good. It’s a observation, not an entry. The sound he expected was the call to prayer from the mosque of Al-Zaytoun, not the muezzin’s voice itself, which could be tinny or beautiful depending on the speaker and the amplifier, but the absence of it. The negative space where that sound had been for thirty-seven years. You don’t wake to a sound. You wake to a silence wearing a sound’s costume.

I scratch it out. The pencil lead snaps.

A commotion at the door. A young man enters, shaking rain from a dark, curly mop. He is all angles and enthusiasm, clutching a stack of books to his chest. He sees the woman with the scarf, his face brightens.

“Tante Elise!”

He bends to kiss her cheeks. The ritual. One, two. The murmur of greeting. He pulls out the chair, sits, begins talking with his hands, his words a rapid, melodic stream I cannot quite catch. His French is native, effortless. He is of this place. His energy is a disturbance in the café’s still air, a splash of cold, real water. He laughs. A bright, unselfconscious sound.

I look away, but the sound hooks into me. It pulls up a memory, not an image, but a kinetic sense: running down a steep, cobbled street in the rain, shoes slipping, Ibrahim laughing behind me, a laugh so big it swallowed the storm, both of us soaked to the skin and late for a lecture we didn’t care about. The memory is whole and sudden, a bubble rising from a deep, black well. It pops against the present. The café solidifies around me again, harder, colder. The taste of the Gauloise is now ash.

Ibrahim is not here. Ibrahim is in a cell, or a grave, or crossing a desert in the back of a truck. I do not know. The last message was eighteen months ago, a single line on a secure platform: The library is burning. Save the index cards. Our code. The library was our circle. The index cards were the people. Save the people. I got out. With the help of a French cultural attaché who liked an essay I’d written on Al-Maʿarri. A lifeline thrown across the sea. I took it. I saved one index card. Myself.

The young man at the next table is arguing about Godard. His aunt is smiling, nodding, sipping her noisette. Their world is intact, a planet with its own gravity, its own debates. I am a speck passing through its atmosphere, burning up silently.

I pay. I gather my papers, my ledger of absence, my false novel. The waiter takes the money with a nod. No au revoir. Outside, the liar’s light has been defeated by a fine, persistent rain. It falls in a mist, beading on the wool of my coat. I turn left, with no destination. This is the ritual: walk until the body is tired enough to believe the mind can sleep.

The streets are a grammar I am slowly learning. The boulevards are declarative sentences. The alleys are subordinate clauses. The Seine is a long, muddy conjunction, flowing between what was and what is. I cross a bridge. The water is the color of lead, of old mirrors. I stop at the midpoint, lean on the stone balustrade. The rain peppers the surface, erasing reflections.

Downstream, the towers of Notre Dame are shrouded in scaffold. A restoration. A putting-back-together. I think of our own cathedral, the Umayyad Mosque. I saw a photo in a news agency window last week. A hole in the wall. Dust. No scaffold. Only ruin, and the more profound ruin to come.

A tourist couple stops near me, consulting a map. American. Their voices carry in the damp air.

“I think it’s back that way. Toward the big arch thing.”

“The Arc de Triomphe, honey. It’s not an ‘arch thing.’”

“Whatever. I’m soaked. Let’s just find the metro.”

They move on. Their conversation, so mundane, so full of the privilege of trivial discomfort, is like a folk song from a distant, unimaginable country. My discomfort is not trivial. It is the ground I stand on. It is the air.

I pull the collar of my coat up. My hand brushes against the small, hard rectangle in my inner pocket. My passport. The French one. A marvel of burgundy leather and gold lettering. République Française. Inside, my new name. Karim Bensaïd. A good name. A safe name. It sits on the tongue lightly. My old name, the one on the index card I did not save, was a weight, a history, a target. I had to let it go. The man at the embassy explained it gently. “It is for your protection. And the protection of those you left behind.” A clean severance. A surgical ghosthood.

But a name is not a shirt you change. It is the skin. This new skin is thin. I feel everything through it—the cold, the rain, the stares that linger a second too long. And underneath, the old skin still aches, a phantom limb of identity.

I walk. Past bookstalls shuttered against the rain. Past a fromagerie with pyramids of chalky cheese. Past a pharmacy with its glowing green cross. The city is a spectacle of being. It simply is, with a dense, self-assured materiality. My city now is a phantom city. It exists in negative, superimposed on these streets. The smell of bread here triggers the memory of bread there—flatter, denser, topped with za’atar. The sound of a motorbike backfiring is, for a heart-stopping second, something else entirely.

This is the exile’s double vision. We are haunted by a place that still exists, but not for us. We are living posthumously in our own lives.

My feet take me to the Luxembourg Gardens. The gates are still open. I enter. The rain has thinned the crowds. A few hardy nannies push prams under black umbrellas. An old man feeds pigeons from a bag of crumbs. The geometric gravel paths are dark with wet. The lawns are an impossibly vivid green, a green that seems to shout its own fertility.

I find an empty chair beneath a bare chestnut tree. The metal is cold and wet through my trousers. I don’t care. I sit. I watch the palace at the far end, its pale stone dim in the grey light.

This is where I am supposed to find beauty. To be inspired. To write the great exile novel. That is the bargain, isn’t it? You lose your home, you gain your depth. Your tragedy is your material. Suffering is currency. Exchange your pain for art. The romantic fallacy of the displaced.

But my pain is not a metaphor. It is a simple, daily machinery. It is checking the news site with a knot in the stomach. It is the inability to call your mother on her birthday. It is the calculation in every new friendship: how much can I tell? Where does the story become a burden? It is the shame of being safe when others are not. It is the relentless translation happening in the back of the skull, a constant, low hum: How do I say this? What is the equivalent? There is no equivalent.

A pigeon struts near my foot, cocking its head. It expects nothing from me. I have no crumbs.

I think of the café, the young man’s laugh. The vitality of it. My own laugh is a forgotten object in a drawer. What do I have in its place? This interior monologue, this endless, looping commentary. This is my medium now. Not the clean sentences of my academic work. This stream. This muddied, recursive, undirected flow. Consciousness as a condition. A weather system inside the skull.

It is getting dark. The street lamps around the garden blink on, their light swallowed by the mist, creating haloes of lonely yellow. The keeper will come soon to ring his bell, to close the gates. The day is ending. Another day in the afterlife.

I stand. My body is stiff. I turn my back on the palace, on the ordered flowerbeds, and walk toward the gate, toward the narrow street that leads to my rented room. A room with a desk, a narrow bed, a hotplate. A cell of possibilities. A chamber for the ghost to haunt.

As I reach the gate, I hear music. A strain of an oud, tentative, searching. It comes from a side street. A few notes, then silence. Then a scale, practiced, melancholic. My breath catches. I stand still, listening. It is not a recording. It is live. Someone here, in this city of stone and rain, is playing an oud.

The notes hang in the damp air, a fragile thread. A question.

I do not follow the sound. I am afraid of what, or who, I might find. Afraid it will be a French jazz musician, experimenting with “world music.” Afraid it will be an old man from Aleppo, whose eyes will meet mine and know me instantly, and the recognition will be a wound.

I turn the other way. I walk quickly, the music fading behind me, replaced by the sound of my own footsteps on the wet pavement, a steady, solitary meter.

But the hook is set. The question of those notes vibrates in the hollow of my chest.

Who?

The first word of my real novel.

Chapter 2

CHAPTER 2

The room smells of damp wool and yesterday’s coffee. A slant of pale, Parisian morning light cuts across the floor, illuminating a topography of dust. I am at the desk, but I am not writing. I am holding the oud note from last night in my mind, trying to fix its frequency before it dissolves into the noise of the day.

It’s gone. Only the echo of the echo remains.

Instead, I am reading a letter. Not a real letter. An email printed out on cheap paper at the internet café on Rue Monge. The text is a bland forest of pleasantries, but between the lines, in the choice of a word, the rhythm of a sentence, I am trying to read the grounds.

Dear Karim,

I hope this finds you well and settling into your new surroundings. Paris in the spring must be inspiring. We continue our work here, though the context remains challenging. The cultural ministry is, as ever, a labyrinth. Remember the archives we discussed? They remain inaccessible, but I make inquiries when possible. I trust your research proceeds. The perspective of an outsider looking back can be most illuminating. Stay focused on your project. It is your anchor.

Warmly,

Claude

Claude is the attaché. My benefactor. His sentences are diplomatic compounds, carefully engineered to say nothing and everything. The context remains challenging. That means arrests have continued. The archives… inaccessible. Our friends are gone, silenced, or worse. The perspective of an outsider. He is reminding me of my role: observe, record, but from here. Do not engage. It is your anchor. A warning. Do not drift into dangerous currents.

I fold the paper, tuck it into the Divine Comedy on my shelf. An appropriate hiding place. My own midway journey.

My project. The anchor. The novel that isn’t. I open the notebook. The blank page is a white desert. I pick up a pen. Not a pencil. Ink is commitment. Or a performance of commitment.

I write: The man who sold pastries had a mustache like a parenthesis around a smile.)

It’s a memory. Abu Walid, from the corner near the university. His knafeh was soaked in rosewater syrup. He always gave students an extra piece. For the brain, he’d say. It needs sugar to fight all those books. Where is Abu Walid now? Is his shop rubble? Is his mustache grey? Is he even alive?

I am not writing a novel. I am taking inventory.

A sound from the apartment below. The scrape of a chair. A muffled voice on the telephone. Madame Céline, the widow who owns the building. She speaks in a rapid, metallic clip. She is suspicious of me. A single man, foreign, quiet. I represent a decline in the neighborhood’s character. Last week, she asked if I was a student. I said I was a writer. Her lips pursed. Ah. The word hung in the air, heavy with connotations of unpaid rent and bohemian disorder. Be careful of the wiring, she said. It is old. Do not overload the circuits.

I am careful not to overload the circuits.

The need for movement seizes me. The room is too small, the ceiling too low. I shrug on my coat. I do not have a destination, but I have a pattern. The café, the gardens, the river. The triangle of my exile.

Today, I break the pattern.

I walk east, away from the Seine, into the tighter, narrower streets of the 5th. The shops change. More tabacs, more pressings, more windows filled with cheap electronics. The faces change too. More color, more languages layered under the French. Arabic. Tamazight. Wolof. A fragment of a familiar curse word, spoken with a laugh, hits me like a slap. I keep my head down.

I find myself in a small square, a place, with a shuttered fountain in the center. On one side is a mosque, a modest structure with a simple minaret. Not the Grand Mosque, just a neighborhood one. Men are coming out, the Friday prayer ending. They spill onto the pavement, shaking hands, embracing. A buzz of conversation. They are of all ages, all shades. Their dress is a mix: traditional djellabas and prayer caps, puffy jackets and jeans. They are here. They belong to this square, to this city, in a way I cannot fathom.

I stand across the street, in the doorway of a closed bakery, watching. A voyeur of belonging.

Two men break from the group, lighting cigarettes. They speak Arabic, but it is a Maghrebi dialect, sharp and sibilant. Not my dialect. Still, the music of it is a key that fits a rusty lock in my chest. I understand them. They are talking about a football match. About a government paperwork delay. Ordinary complaints. The liturgy of daily life.

One of them, an older man with a kind, weary face, catches my eye. He nods, a brief, neutral acknowledgment. A man standing in a doorway is not an unusual sight. I nod back, too quickly, and look away. My heart is pounding. The simple recognition, the faintest thread of connection, feels illicit. Dangerous.

I move on, my legs unsteady. The encounter, a non-encounter really, has left me shaken. It is the oud note made flesh. A reminder that the world I come from is not an abstract memory. It is a living, breathing, praying reality, even here, especially here. And I am outside it. I am Karim Bensaïd, French passport, writer of a non-novel. I am a ghost with paperwork.

I walk without seeing, turned inward. When I look up, I am on a broad, busy street lined with fabric shops. Bolts of cloth spill onto the sidewalk: vibrant ankara prints, shimmering brocades, utilitarian linens. The signs are in Arabic and French. This is a different kind of diaspora. West African, mostly. The energy is different—louder, more commercial. Music thumps from a speaker, a fast, percussive beat.

I am utterly dislocated. This is not my fragment of the shattered mirror. This is another fragment entirely. We are all exiles here, but our exiles do not recognize each other. We are islands in the same archipelago, separated by different seas.

A hunger twists in my stomach, sharp and sudden. Not for food, but for a specific taste. For the food of there. I have been eating baguettes and cheese, drinking coffee. Subsisting. But now, a craving asserts itself, a deep, cellular demand.

I begin to look for a restaurant. Not a Frenchified “oriental” place with laminated menus. A real one. I follow my nose, literally, sniffing the air for cumin, for coriander, for the scent of charcoal-grilled meat.

I find it down an alley. A simple sign: Restaurant Le Levant. The window is steamy. Inside, I can see a few Formica tables. I push the door open.

A bell jingles. The warmth hits me first, thick with the smells of garlic, frying onions, lamb, and something else—the unmistakable, dusty-sweet smell of dried mint. It is a Proustian cannonball. For a moment, I am in my grandmother’s kitchen. I am ten years old. The world is whole.

A man behind a small counter looks up. He is large, with a shaved head and a thick mustache. He wipes his hands on a towel.

“Bonjour. Une personne?”

I almost answer in Arabic. I stop myself. “Oui. S’il vous plaît.”

He gestures to a small table against the wall. I sit. The placemat is a map of Lebanon. Idiotic. But the menu, when he brings it, is handwritten in Arabic and French. The script is familiar, looping, generous.

My eyes scan the list. Foul. Hummus. Moutabal. Kibbeh. Kafta. Each word is a spell. I want to order everything. To eat the entire alphabet of home.

The man returns. “Vous avez choisi?”

I point. “Foul. Et kibbeh nayyeh. S’il vous plaît.”

He raises an eyebrow. Kibbeh nayyeh is raw lamb, spiced. It is a test. A tourist doesn’t order it. A homesick person does.

“Avec tout? Oignons, menthe, huile d’olive?”

“Avec tout,” I say, my voice thick.

He nods, a flicker of something in his eyes. Not warmth, exactly. Recognition. He turns and calls the order through a bead curtain into the kitchen. In Arabic. “Foul wa kibbeh nayyeh, bil-kull.” The words are a balm.

I wait. The restaurant is empty except for an old man in the corner sipping tea and reading a newspaper. The radio plays softly—an Arabic pop song, saccharine and full of longing. I let it wash over me. I am not trying to translate. I am just listening to the river of the language.

The food comes. The foul is in a shallow bowl, beans swimming in tahini and lemon, topped with a confetti of parsley, tomato, and raw onion. The kibbeh nayyeh is a mound of finely ground meat, red and fragrant with pepper and allspice, drizzled with green olive oil. There is a stack of warm pita bread.

My hands tremble slightly as I tear a piece of bread, scoop up some foul. I bring it to my mouth. The taste is not memory. It is stronger. It is the thing itself. The creamy beans, the sharp lemon, the earthy cumin. It is perfect. It is a fact.

I eat slowly, methodically. Each bite is a reclamation. A tiny, edible piece of territory. For these minutes, I am not in exile. I am at a table, eating food I understand.

The man behind the counter is watching me. When I finish, he brings a small glass of tea, unsweetened, with a sprig of mint.

“Min wayn?” he asks quietly. Where are you from?

The question, in Arabic, hangs in the air. It is the most dangerous question in the world. I look at him. His face is neutral, but his eyes are not. They are the eyes of a man who has also left, who also knows the price of a direct answer.

I give the safe, false answer. The one from the passport. “I am French.”

He nods slowly. He understands the code. He asked in Arabic, I answered in evasion. The dance of the displaced.

“But your parents?” he persists, gently.

“From the Levant,” I say. A region, not a country. A vague, bruised geography.

This satisfies him. It is enough common ground, enough shared ambiguity. “The food was good?”

“It was correct,” I say, using the French word correct, which means decent, okay. But in my tone, I put the Arabic meaning: it was right. It was proper.

He hears it. A faint smile touches his mustache. “Al-hamdu lillah,” he says. Praise God.

I pay. As I am leaving, he speaks again. “We are here every day. Except Sunday.”

It is an invitation. Or a statement of existence. I nod. “Shukran.”

Outside, the cold air is a shock. The taste of cumin and mint is still on my tongue, a ghost limb of flavor. I feel both nourished and eviscerated. The meal was a homecoming to a home that no longer exists. It was proof of both presence and absence.

I walk back toward my room, but the coordinates have shifted. The city now contains a point of reference. A small, steamy room with a map of Lebanon for a placemat. An anchor, but not the one Claude meant.

When I get back to my street, I see Madame Céline talking to a policeman. They are standing by her door. She is gesticulating. He is writing in a notebook. My blood turns to ice. I slow my pace. I consider walking past, but it is my street. My door is just beyond them.

As I approach, Madame Céline sees me. She points. The policeman turns. He is young, with a bored, efficient face.

“Monsieur Bensaïd?” he says.

“Oui.”

“A moment, please.” He asks for my papers. My heart is a frantic animal in my chest. I hand over the burgundy passport. He examines it, compares the photo to my face. He writes down the number. Madame Céline watches, her arms crossed.

“There was an incident last night,” the policeman says, handing back the passport. “A break-in attempt two streets over. Madame is concerned about security. You did not see or hear anything unusual?”

“No. Nothing.”

“You work at home, Madame says. You are often here during the day.”

It is not a question. It is an observation. My alibi is my solitude. “I write. I am here most days.”

He nods, closes his notebook. “Remain vigilant. Report anything suspicious.” He tips his cap to Madame Céline and walks away.

She looks at me. “It is not personal, Monsieur Bensaïd. But we must be careful. The neighborhood… it changes.”

“I understand,” I say. My voice is calm. The calm of sheer terror repressed.

Inside my room, I lock the door. I lean against it, breathing heavily. The encounter with the policeman was routine. A formality. But for me, it was an earthquake. The state, in the form of a bored young man, held my identity in his hands. He found it correct. But the scrutiny, the notation, the fact of being noted—it stripped away the thin film of safety.

The taste of kibbeh nayyeh is gone. In its place, a metallic fear.

I go to the window, peek through the shutters. The street is empty. Madame Céline has gone inside.

I take Claude’s email from the Divine Comedy. I read it again. It is your anchor.

He is right. The novel is not a pretense. It is a necessity. It is the only activity that is beyond suspicion. A writer writing. A harmless, introspective pursuit.

I sit at the desk. I open the notebook to a fresh page. I pick up the pen.

This time, I do not write a memory. I write a sentence of fiction. A sentence that could only be written here, now, by me.

The exile’s first lesson is that the past is not a country you can visit. It is a language you are slowly forgetting, and every word you remember is pronounced with an accent.

It’s a start. Not an anchor, perhaps. But a line cast into the dark water.

Chapter 3

CHAPTER 3

The dream is always the same. I am in the old apartment, the one with the blue-tiled balcony overlooking the street of silverware vendors. The air is hot and still. I am looking for something urgent, something I must take with me. I open drawers: they are full of dry leaves. I check shelves: they hold only dust. A voice, my father’s, calls from another room, but the words are muffled, as if through water. I try to run toward the voice, but my feet are heavy, sinking into the carpet which has become mud. The call to prayer begins, but it is distorted, slow, a record player dying. I wake with a gasp, the taste of mud in my mouth.

It is 4:17 AM. The room is a cave of shadows. The silence is absolute, a pressure on the eardrums. This is the witching hour of exile, when the ghosts are most solid and the present is most faint.

I get up. I make tea on the hotplate. I don’t turn on the main light, just the small lamp on the desk. The pool of yellow light creates a fragile stage. I sit within it.

The dream has left a residue, a psychic grit. The search for the unnamable thing. The voice just out of reach. This is the machinery of loss, grinding away in the subconscious. It does its work in the dark.

I open the notebook. The sentence from yesterday stares back. The exile’s first lesson… It seems pretentious in the raw morning light. Clever. I dislike it. Exile isn’t taught in lessons. It is absorbed, like radiation. It works silently on the bones.

I turn the page. I begin to write, not thinking, just letting the dream’s imagery flow.

The drawers were full of leaves. Not autumn leaves, but dry, papery fig leaves, the ones that leave a white, dusty print on your fingers. Why leaves? What was I supposed to take? A document? A photograph? A key? The leaves crumbled to powder when I touched them. The powder smelled of distance.

Better. It’s not fiction. It’s reportage from the interior.

I write for an hour, until the grey dawn bleeds at the edges of the shutters. The tea grows cold. I fill three pages with disjointed fragments: the mud-carpet, the drowned voice, the silverware vendors whose ting-ting-ting of hammer on metal was the soundtrack of afternoons. I am not crafting a story. I am mapping a ruin.

When the church bells ring for seven, I stop. My hand is cramped. I feel emptied, and somehow cleaner. The dream’s poison is on the page now, not in me.

The encounter with the policeman yesterday has left a new kind of anxiety, a low-grade hum of surveillance. I decide today will be a day of impeccable, boring normality. I will be a model exile. I will go to the library.

The Bibliothèque Sainte-Geneviève is my chosen sanctuary. It is not the grandiose Bibliothèque Nationale, but a scholars’ library, serious and quiet. Its reading room is a temple of knowledge, a long, high-vaulted space with dark wood desks and green lamps. Here, among the earnest students and aging researchers, I am invisible. I belong to the fraternity of the desk-bound.

I take my usual seat at the back. I have a ritual: I request two books. One related to my “research”—today, a volume on Andalusian poetry as a bridge between cultures. The other is something entirely for myself, a secret pleasure. Today, it is an old, illustrated edition of A Thousand and One Nights. The weight of the books, their smell of old paper and glue, is a comfort. It is a tangible connection to a world of ideas that exists beyond borders, beyond politics.

I open the poetry book, take out my notecards, and begin to take notes. The work is real. I was, am, a scholar. The focus is a relief. It requires a different part of the brain than the chaotic stream of consciousness. It requires precision, analysis. For a few hours, I am not Karim the exile. I am Karim the thinker. It is a legitimate passport.

At noon, my concentration breaks. The student next to me, a young woman with severe glasses, unwraps a sandwich. The smell of ham and butter intrudes. I close my eyes, try to summon the smell of foul from yesterday. It’s gone.

I get up to return the books. As I walk along the wrought-iron gallery that overlooks the main floor, I see him.

He is sitting at a table near a pillar, a man in his late forties, with a closely trimmed beard going grey. He is reading a newspaper, but not a French one. It is Al-Hayat, the pan-Arab daily. The familiar script is like a flare in the muted room. He reads with intense concentration, his forehead furrowed.

I stop walking. I am staring. I know it is rude, dangerous, but I cannot look away. He is of my world. Not just the Arab world, but the specific, intellectual, politically-engaged world I came from. The world that is now ash.

As if feeling my gaze, he looks up. Our eyes meet. His are dark, intelligent, tired. There is no flicker of recognition, only a mild query. Do I know you?

I quickly look down and walk on, my heart hammering. I return the books to the cart, my hands unsteady. I should leave. Immediately. This is a complication. A thread that could unravel everything.

But I don’t leave. I go to the catalog, pretending to look for another reference. I watch him from behind a shelf. He folds his newspaper, puts it in a leather satchel. He stands, puts on a neat, wool overcoat. He walks toward the exit with a purposeful, unhurried stride.

A reckless impulse takes hold. I follow.

I keep a safe distance, out on the street. He walks south, toward the Pantheon. He is not window-shopping. He has a destination. He turns onto Rue Clovis. I follow, feeling like a character in a cheap spy novel, but the pull is too strong. He is a cipher, a possible link. To what, I don’t know.

He stops at a small, non-descript hotel. The Hôtel des Écoles. He doesn’t go in. He checks his watch, looks up and down the street. He is waiting.

I duck into a doorway, my pulse loud in my ears. What am I doing? This is madness. Claude’s warning echoes: It is your anchor. Stay focused.

A minute later, a car pulls up. A dark blue Renault. The back door opens. The man with the beard gets in. The car doesn’t pull away immediately. Through the slightly open window, I see him lean forward, talking to the driver.

I can’t see the driver’s face. But I see a hand on the steering wheel. A hand with a distinctive silver ring on the index finger, shaped like a scarab.

I know that ring.

The world tilts. The sounds of the street—the traffic, a distant siren, a child’s laugh—recede into a tunnel of silence.

Ibrahim wore a ring like that. A cheap, tourist-market scarab ring I’d teased him about. You look like a pharaoh of the proletariat, I’d said. He’d laughed. It’s for luck. And it annoys the serious comrades.

It can’t be. It is a coincidence. A common ring.

The car pulls away, merging into traffic. I stand frozen in the doorway, watching it disappear. My breath comes in short gasps. The solid stone of Paris seems to liquefy, to ripple.

It was him. It wasn’t him. The mind races, constructing and demolishing possibilities. If it was Ibrahim, he is alive. He is here. Why? How



---

Chapter 4

CHAPTER 4

The plan was a house of cards built on a bed of ice. We laid it out on the scarred wooden table in Claude’s back room, under the jaundiced glow of a single bulb, the details feeling less like strategy and more like a shared incantation against the dark. A warding. The cold from the street had followed me in, a spectral third at our table, and I felt its fingers on the back of my neck as Claude spoke of alarms, of schedules, of the weight of a man’s routine.

“The villa is here,” he said, his fingertip pressing a map of the 16th arrondissement into submission. “A fortress of bourgeois anxiety. Walls, a gate, a garden that is more geometry than greenery. He spends every Thursday evening at the opera. A ritual. The staff is dismissed by ten. The house is empty, but for the systems. They are… considerable.”

Marlene traced a route with a nicotine-stained finger. “The alley here. Delivery access. The lock is a Chubb, old but proud. Picking it is a conversation, not a break-in. We must be polite.” She said it with the gravity of a priestess. Her tools were laid out on a velvet cloth beside her: slender picks, tension wrenches, a fold of black silk. They were the only clean things in the room.

I listened, but the words slid off me. They were sounds, not sense. The map was a flat, meaningless lie. All I could see was the silver scarab, winking from a steering wheel in the Parisian gloom. A ghost-ring. The memory of Ibrahim’s laugh, a sound I had buried in the same deep pit as the honest cold of the lake, now echoed up through the strata, thin and mocking.

“Leo.” Claude’s voice was a blade, cutting through the fog. “The interior alarm. You are sure of the panel location?”

I blinked. “The foyer. To the right of a hideous marble console. A modern panel. A keypad. It will have a sixty-second grace period from the moment the door is breached.”

“And you can disarm it.”
It wasn’t a question. It was a brick laid into the foundation of our fragile house.
“I can disarm it.”
My voice sounded foreign to me. It was the voice of the man I was pretending to be, a man of useful skills and singular focus. Not the man who had stood shaking in a doorway, watching a ghost drive away.

The planning droned on. Times, signals, fallbacks. A language of precision to cage the chaos. I contributed where required, my old training rising on autopilot. Assess the entry point. Identify the choke points. Plan the egress. It was a mechanical liturgy. But beneath it, a tectonic shift was underway. The mission was no longer an abstract retrieval, a job for Claude. It had become a séance. The villa was not just a house; it was the inmost cave, and whatever lay in its safe—documents, names, a ledger of betrayals—was now secondary. It was the ring. It was the thread that might lead back to a hand I had watched vanish into a crowd in Beirut two years ago, a disappearance so total it had the finality of a gunshot.

I was not going there to steal. I was going there to dig.
  - We moved at dusk, a procession of shadows. Claude drove an unremarkable grey van that smelled of damp vegetables and regret. Marlene sat up front, a silent silhouette, her gaze constantly scanning the mirrors, the side streets, the faces of pedestrians. I was in the back, on a cold metal ledge, feeling every jolt of the cobblestones through my spine.

The silence was a pressure chamber. It was not the quiet of concentration; it was the quiet of three separate storms, each raging behind a wall of glass.

Claude broke it, his eyes on the road. “There is a change. A small one.” He said it to the windshield.
Marlene’s head turned slowly. “What change?”
“The maid. She is not leaving at ten tonight. She has a rendez-vous. She will leave at nine-forty, in a hurry. It gives us twenty more minutes of true emptiness.”
“How do you know this?” I asked.
“The baker’s son is in love with her. He told the florist’s daughter, who told my contact at the tabac.” He shrugged, a Gallic gesture that encompassed the entire, gossiping city. “Paris is a village with better architecture.”

It was a good change. More time was oxygen. Yet, it felt like a crack. Reliance on the whims of a lovestruck boy and a maid’s romantic life. It underscored the absurd fragility of it all. We were not master thieves. We were insects, scuttling between the cracks of a giant’s day, hoping not to be noticed.

“You are quiet, Leo,” Marlene said, not turning around. “Your mind is elsewhere.”
“It’s here,” I said.
“It is not. It is back in that doorway on Rue Clovis.” She finally looked at me, her eyes catching the fleeting glow of a passing streetlamp. They were old eyes, full of a history she never spoke of. “A ghost walked past you. It is a disorienting thing.”
I stiffened. “You don’t know what you’re talking about.”
“I know the look. I have worn it. You see a profile in a crowd that is not there. You hear a laugh in a language no one else speaks. It is the tax of exile. You carry the dead with you, and sometimes, they decide to walk ahead of you, just to remind you they are still your baggage.” She turned back to the window. “Do not let the ghost steer the car tonight. It is a bad driver.”

Her words were a cold splash of water. She had seen it, the fissure in my focus. Claude’s hands tightened on the wheel, his knuckles pale. The tension in the van spiked, a live wire now lying coiled between us.

“Is there something you need to say, Leo?” Claude’s voice was dangerously calm. “Is your anchor not holding?”
It is your anchor. Stay focused. His earlier warning, now an accusation.
The ghost. The anchor. They were at war in my chest. “The anchor holds,” I lied, the words ash in my mouth. “I saw a ring. It reminded me of someone. That’s all.”
“A ring,” Claude repeated flatly.
“On the driver. The man with the beard got into a car. The driver wore a scarab ring. A cheap thing. Someone I knew… wore one like it.”
“And this someone is dead?” Marlene asked.
“I believed so.”
“Believed.” Claude let the word hang. “And now this belief is shaken. And this shaken belief is in the van with us.”
The accusation was now naked. I was the crack. I was the variable. I was the personal, messy, human flaw in his clean, professional equation.

“It changes nothing about tonight,” I said, forcing steel into my voice. “The job is the job.”
“The job requires a clear mind,” Claude countered. “Not one chasing phantoms. Phantoms get people killed. They get me killed.”
“Then stop the van,” I snapped, the storm breaking through. “Leave me here. Go do it yourselves. See how far you get with the alarm panel without me.”
The silence returned, thicker now, poisoned. We were a diaspora of three, thrown together not by blood or belief, but by need and nebulous debt. There was no trust here, only a temporary alignment of interests. Marlene’s warning had been a kindness, but it had exposed the rot. We drove on, the only sound the growl of the engine and the tense, shallow rhythm of our breathing. The villa awaited, a silent maw. We were already in its throat.
  - The villa was not merely a house; it was a statement in limestone and wrought iron. It crouched behind its high wall on a quiet, tree-lined street, exuding a smug, impregnable silence. The wealth here was not flamboyant; it was deep, sedimentary, layered over generations. It did not shout. It simply ignored.

We observed from the shadows of a small park across the street, three statues pretending to be part of the landscape. The last of the evening’s light had been swallowed by a bruised, purple twilight. Windows in the villa began to glow, one by one, a mechanical heartbeat. At precisely nine-forty, a side gate opened, and a young woman in a coat hurried out, her steps quick and light. The maid. The baker’s son’s good fortune. The house’s final, living breath exhaled into the night.

We waited ten endless minutes. No more lights came on. No shadows moved behind the windows. The villa was a shell.

“Now,” Claude whispered.

We crossed the street, not running, but moving with the swift, fluid purpose of water finding a drain. Marlene went to work on the delivery gate lock. She was a artist. There was no violence, no scratching. She bent close, her ear almost to the metal, her hands moving her tools with a gentle, precise intimacy. I heard a series of soft, metallic sighs. A click, deeper and fuller than the others. She pushed the gate open a silent inch, nodded.

We slipped through, into a narrow, paved corridor smelling of damp stone and garbage bins. The back of the villa loomed. A kitchen door, solid oak. Marlene repeated her ritual. This lock was newer, more complex. A minute passed. Two. My nerves were live wires, sparking in the damp air. Every second was a drumbeat. Claude was a tense presence at my shoulder, his breath controlled, his impatience a physical heat.

Another click. Softer. The door yielded.

The darkness inside was absolute, a velvet weight. We stood in what felt like a pantry, the air smelling of bleach and stale herbs. I pulled the penlight from my pocket, cupping its beam. A sliver of light pointed the way to the kitchen proper, a cavern of stainless steel and marble.

Then, a low, electronic hum. A tiny red eye winking to life on a panel by the inner door. The grace period had begun.
Sixty seconds.
“Go,” Claude breathed.

I moved, the beam of my light dancing over polished floors. Foyer. Marble console, hideous as promised. The panel glowed a soft, malevolent green. I was at its side, my fingers finding the keypad. The code was a string of numbers Claude’s contact had procured—the birthday of the owner’s prized poodle. The absurdity of it almost made me laugh. The security of empires, undone by canine sentimentality.

My fingers stabbed the digits. 1-9-0-8-1-3.
The green light held.
Fifty seconds.
I pressed ENTER.
Nothing.
The green light stared back, impassive.
A cold trickle of sweat traced my spine. I entered the code again, slower, deliberate.
1. 9. 0. 8. 1. 3. ENTER.
The green light blinked. Once. Twice. Then it shifted to a steady, forgiving blue. A soft thunk echoed from within the walls as the magnetic locks disengaged. The house sighed, its defenses standing down.

I let out a breath I hadn’t known I was holding. Claude and Marlene materialized beside me. “Good,” Claude murmured, the single word heavy with relief. “The study is upstairs. First door on the left at the top.”

We ascended a curved staircase, our steps muffled by a thick runner. The villa’s opulence unfolded in the jagged slices of my penlight: gilt-framed portraits of stern men, a vase taller than a child, the cold glitter of a chandelier. It was a museum to a life I despised, a life of insulated, curated comfort. It was everything I had fled, yet rendered in a different, more polished dialect.

The study door was locked. Marlene went to work again. This was faster. The door swung inward on silent hinges.

The room smelled of leather, old paper, and a faint, expensive cigar smoke that had woven itself into the fabric of the curtains. A massive desk dominated the space, a monolith of dark wood. The safe was behind a painting—a predictable pastoral scene. Claude moved the painting aside. The safe was a modern digital model, a sleek, grey face.

“Marlene,” he said.

She stepped forward, but not with her picks. She produced a small, sophisticated device from her bag, connecting it to the safe’s keypad with delicate leads. “This will listen to the internal mechanism,” she whispered. “It will tell us the combination. It will take time. Five minutes, perhaps ten. Watch the door.”

Claude took up a position by the study entrance, a shadow among shadows. I was to watch the window overlooking the back garden. I stood there, but I wasn’t seeing the manicured hedges, the empty stone patio. I was seeing the scarab ring. I was hearing Marlene’s words. Do not let the ghost steer the car.

The ghost wasn’t steering. It was waiting. And this room, this temple of secrets, was where it wanted to be. My eyes scanned the desk. Neat stacks of correspondence. A silver letter opener. A globe on a stand. And a photograph in a simple silver frame. I tilted my penlight toward it.

It was a group photograph, taken at some sun-drenched resort. The villa’s owner, a man with a carefully trimmed beard and the smile of a well-fed shark, stood with his arm around a younger man. The younger man was laughing, squinting against the sun. On his hand, on his index finger, was a blur of silver.

My heart stopped.
I picked up the frame.
The photograph was sharper under direct light. The ring was unmistakable. A scarab. Not identical to Ibrahim’s—this one was larger, more ornate, truly silver—but the kinship was undeniable. It was the same breed of symbol. A brother to the ghost.

And the young man wearing it… I knew the slope of his shoulders. The way his head tilted when he laughed. It was not Ibrahim. It was someone else. Someone from before. A face from the crowded, smoky rooms of the university in Beirut, a hanger-on, a messenger boy for one of the factions. His name surfaced from the murk: Karim. Faint, insignificant.

What was Karim doing in a photograph with a French industrialist with suspected arms deals?
The connection was a bolt of lightning, fusing disparate worlds. The ghost had not been Ibrahim. It had been a fingerprint. A signature. A style. The ring was not a personal quirk; it was a marker. A badge.

“Leo.” Claude’s voice was a razor in the quiet. “What are you doing?”
I couldn’t put the frame down. “This photograph. The man with the owner. He is wearing a scarab ring.”
“So?”
“The driver today wore one. This man…” I struggled to assemble the logic, the pattern forming in the chaos. “He was from Beirut. He was connected. To people I knew. To Ibrahim.”
Claude was across the room in three swift strides. He took the photograph from my hands, looked at it, his face a mask. “This is a coincidence. A rich man’s souvenir. It means nothing.”
“It means everything,” I hissed, keeping my voice low. “It’s a link. The driver works for someone connected to this man. This man is connected to Beirut. To my past. This isn’t just about your industrialist’s dirty deals, Claude. This goes deeper. This touches the thing that unmade me.”
The revelation was a key turning in a lock deep inside me. The mission was no longer Claude’s. It was mine. The safe wasn’t just holding documents; it was holding an explanation. A reason. A name.

Marlene’s device beeped, a soft, triumphant chirp. A series of numbers glowed on its tiny screen. She turned to the safe, her movements swift. She entered the code. A heavy clunk echoed in the silent room. The safe door swung open.

Claude shoved the photograph into my hands and went to the safe. He pulled out folders, a small ledger, a sealed envelope. He scanned them quickly in the beam of his own light. “Contracts. Shipment manifests. Names. It’s all here.” He began stuffing them into a flat portfolio.

My eyes were locked on the dark cavity of the safe. There was something else in there, lying flat on the bottom. Not a document. A small, wooden box, inlaid with mother-of-pearl. An old, personal thing. Out of place among the sterile files.

“Wait,” I said.
Claude ignored me, zipping the portfolio.
I reached past him, into the safe, and took the box. It was light. I opened it.

Inside, on a bed of faded velvet, were not jewels. They were pins. Lapel pins. Five of them. Each was a tiny, exquisite silver scarab beetle.

And beneath them, a small, black-and-white photograph, curled at the edges. A photograph I knew. It was of a group of students, standing on the Corniche in Beirut, the Mediterranean a bright blur behind them. I was in that photograph. Younger, squinting, my arm around Ibrahim. He was smiling, his head thrown back. And on his lapel, clearly visible, was one of the silver scarab pins.

The world did not tilt this time. It shattered.
It was not a ring. It was a cell. A network. A symbol of affiliation. Ibrahim had not worn a cheap tourist trinket for luck. He had worn a pin. A pin identical to those in this box, in this safe, in this villa of a French arms dealer. The ring on the driver’s hand, the ring in the photograph, they were the civilian cousins of this badge.

Ibrahim had been part of this. Connected to this. The ground fell away. The man I had loved, the idealist with the quick laugh and the dog-eared books of poetry, had been… what? An asset? A contact? A fool?

Claude snatched the box from my hands, looked at the pins, at the photograph. His face, for the first time, showed genuine shock. Then it hardened into something colder than anger. “You. You are in this photograph.”
“Yes.”
“Your friend. He is one of them.”
“I don’t know what ‘them’ is,” I whispered, the truth tearing out of me.
“This changes everything,” Claude said, his voice low and venomous. “You are not a hired hand. You are a variable I did not account for. You are connected to the target. You have brought your war into my operation.”
“I didn’t know.”
“It doesn’t matter!” He shoved the box into the portfolio. “We are leaving. Now.”
But it was too late.
Downstairs, a door clicked shut.
A heavy, deliberate step sounded in the hall.
Then another.
Someone was home.



---

Chapter 5

CHAPTER 5

The footsteps on the marble were a measured punctuation to the silence that had swallowed the villa. Not a rush, not a search. A claim. Each one was a period at the end of a sentence we had not meant to write. Claude’s face was the colour of old wax. He did not look at me, not at the box, not at the photograph that had unstitched the world. He looked at the door of the study, his hand moving to the small of his back, beneath the line of his tailored jacket. The gesture was smooth, practiced, a comma in the violence he was preparing to write.

Ibrahim’s smile in the photograph was a brand on my vision. The scarab on his lapel was no longer a piece of jewellery. It was an eye, watching me from across the years, from inside the memory I had curated and polished like a stone from the shore. I had carried that memory into exile. It was one of the few things I had brought that was not sharp. Now it had grown a carapace, and legs, and it was crawling up my spine.

“The service stairs,” Léa breathed. She was a silhouette against the window, all taut lines. “Through the dressing room.”

Claude shook his head once, sharp. “He will have come in through the kitchen. The stairs exit there. We are in the bottle.” His eyes finally cut to me. The cold in them was the Parisian kind, the sly, insinuating kind. I had brought the lake’s cleaver into his genteel trap, and now the glass was cracking. “You will speak,” he said. It was not a request.

The footsteps stopped outside the study door.

A key slid into the lock. The sound was obscenely loud, a metallic sigh.

The door opened.

He was not what I expected. The footsteps had conjured a giant, a brute in a uniform. The man who stood in the doorway was of medium height, trim, dressed in a charcoal sweater and trousers that spoke of quiet money. His hair was silver, swept back from a high forehead. He held a leather folio in one hand, keys in the other. He looked like a retired professor of philosophy, the kind who gives erudite, melancholy interviews on public radio. Only his eyes gave him away. They were the colour of a winter sky over the Channel, and they took in the scene—the open safe, Claude standing rigid, Léa by the window, me with the empty box in my hands—with a single, sweeping glance. There was no surprise in them. Only a profound, weary calculation.

“Monsieur Valois,” he said. His voice was a dry rustle, like pages turning. “You are early. And you have brought guests. How… democratic.”

Claude’s posture shifted, a fractional softening. The performance began. “Alain. A misunderstanding. A fault in the alarm system. We were passing, saw the signal was in error, and used the override to ensure your property was secure.”

Alain. Alain Mercier. The name from the dossier. The man who moved things between shadows.

Mercier placed his folio on a side table, as if settling in for a tutorial. “And the contents of my safe? Part of your security audit?” His gaze drifted to me. It lingered on my face, then dropped to the box in my hands. To the photograph I still clutched, my fingers gone numb. His winter eyes did not change, but something in the room did. The temperature dropped another degree. It was the chill of recognition.

“You have an eye for curios, mademoiselle,” he said. “Those are rather rare. May I?”

He held out a hand. It was steady. I had no choice. I crossed the room, the Persian rug swallowing the sound of my boots, and placed the box in his palm. Our fingers did not touch. He looked at the pins, at the photograph nestled among them. He sighed, a sound of genuine disappointment.

“The past is such a careless tenant,” he said. “It leaves its rubbish in the oddest corners.” He looked from the photograph to my face, comparing. “You knew him. Ibrahim al-Masri.”

It was not a question. The way he said the name—the precise, unaccented Arabic—sent a fresh crack through me. This man had not just known of Ibrahim. He had known him.

“What was he to you?” The words were out, rough, before I could stop them.

Claude made a low, warning sound in his throat. Mercier ignored him.

“A idealist,” Mercier said, with a slight shrug. “A poet. A node. A very promising one, for a time. He had a gift for connection. For making people believe in a common thread.” His thumb stroked the edge of the photograph. “He believed the scarab was a symbol of transformation. Of rolling the weight of the old world into something new. Charming. We indulged him. Symbols are useful glue for the young and passionate.”

Each word was a needle, sewing my old love into a shroud of cold utility. “He was not one of you.”

“One of us?” Mercier almost smiled. “There is no ‘us,’ mademoiselle. There is only the market. The market for influence, for security, for outcome. He was a supplier. Of information. Of perspective. He moved in circles we could not. He believed he was using us to funnel support to his cause. A common delusion. The arrow always believes it is flying of its own volition. It does not feel the string.”

I thought of Ibrahim’s fervour, his whispered plans in darkened Beirut apartments, the maps he drew with a finger in the spilt coffee. The secret funds he’d alluded to, the ‘sympathisers’ in Europe. I had thought it naivety. It was a transaction. He had been bartering his access, his authenticity, for scraps from this man’s table. The poetry, the laughter, the love—had that been part of the cover? Or had it been the one true thing, the human thing, he’d tried to protect from the market? The question was a vortex. I could not look at it directly.

“You killed him.” My voice was flat. A stone dropped into a well.

Mercier looked genuinely pained. “The market corrected. His position became… untenable. A liability. He was sentimental. He began to question the terms of trade. To speak of debts, of morality.” He said the word as if it were a quaint, obsolete dialect. “Sentimentality is a flaw in the product. It was not a personal matter.”

Not a personal matter. My Ibrahim, with his specific smell of coffee and old books, the scar on his knee from a childhood fall, his terrible singing voice in the shower—reduced to a flawed product, corrected by the market. The exile’s grief is a private, frozen thing. This was a public dissection. My war, as Claude had called it, was not a war at all. It was a failed business deal.

Claude had heard enough. “Alain. This is a complication we did not need. The woman is a contractor. She was not vetted for this… history. We will leave. The matter of the shipment—”

“Is now infinitely more complex,” Mercier finished for him. He placed the box back in the safe, closed the door, and spun the dial. The scarabs were gone, returned to their velvet tomb. But the photograph he kept, folding it once and slipping it into his breast pocket. A receipt. “She is not a contractor, Claude. She is a witness. And she is connected. The connection is dead, but connections have ghosts. Ghosts have weight. They unbalance equations.”

He turned those winter eyes on me fully. “You came for a ring. You found a hive. Do you know what happens to curious things that stumble into a hive?”

Léa moved then. It was a blur of grey. She did not go for a weapon. She kicked the small table with the porcelain lamp between Mercier and the door. It shattered in a cascade of white shards and noise, a temporary wall of distraction. “Now!” she hissed.

Claude was already moving, not toward the door, but toward the window behind the desk—the same window that had given us the honeyed, lying light. He wrenched it open. Cold air, real and sharp, rushed in, scattering papers. “Down the pipe. Go!”

Mercier did not shout. He simply stepped back into the hallway and called out, calm and clear. “Anton. The study. All of them.”

A heavy tread answered from downstairs.

Léa went out the window first, grabbing the thick drainpipe with a grunt, swinging her legs into the void. I followed. The metal was icy, biting through my gloves. I did not look down. I looked back into the room. Claude was pulling a sleek, black pistol from his back. He did not aim it at Mercier. He aimed it at me.

“The photograph,” he said. “Give it to me.”

I hung there, suspended between the lie of the room and the hard truth of the drop. “He has it.”

“The other one. In your pocket. You took it from the box. Do not play the fool with me.”

My blood went still. In the shock, my hand had acted on its own, folding the duplicate photograph I’d seen beneath the pins—a closer shot, just Ibrahim and me, laughing, the scarab a bright spot on his coat—and slipping it into my inner pocket. A thief’s reflex. A lover’s reflex.

Mercier’s eyebrows lifted slightly. “Two copies. Sentimentality upon sentimentality. How very redundant.”

The heavy tread was on the stairs now. Claude’s finger tightened on the trigger. His alliance, his cold professionalism, had evaporated. I was a variable. A flawed product. “It is not yours to keep,” he snarled.

I let go of the pipe.

I fell one floor, maybe less, into a thick, unkempt boxwood hedge. It clawed at my clothes, snapped beneath me, the branches a brutal parody of embrace. The air left my lungs in a silent rush. Léa was already up, pulling me out by the collar. “Run. To the wall. Left.”

We ran. The garden was a formal geometry of shadow and dormant rose beds. Behind us, a shout, then the flat, ugly crack of a pistol shot. It wasn’t Claude’s. It was heavier. Anton. The sound was a physical thing, slapping against the back walls of the villas. A security light blazed on somewhere, then another, turning the garden into a stark theatre of fleeing shapes.

The wall was eight feet high, topped with broken glass. Léa made a stirrup with her hands. I stepped, pushed, scrambled, felt glass bite into my palm as I hauled myself over. I dropped onto the hard-packed dirt of an alley. Léa landed beside me a second later, a cat-like roll, coming up running.

We did not speak. We ran through a maze of service alleys, past overflowing bins and startled cats, the taste of petrol and rotting vegetables thick in the air. The sirens began in the distance, a wailing chorus drawn by the gunshot. They were not for us. They were part of the background noise, the city’s constant exhalation. We were a momentary dissonance, already fading.

We stopped, finally, in the dripping stone archway of a porte cochère, lungs burning. The cold here was different again. It was the cold of the unowned spaces, the cracks between homes. It had no pretence of warmth. It was simply absence.

Léa leaned against the wall, head back, breathing hard. She looked at my bleeding hand. “You are lucky. It could have been your throat.”

“He was going to shoot me. Claude.”

“Yes. You became a ledger entry. A liability. You saw his face when he saw that photograph. You contaminated the operation with a personal debt. In our work, personal debts are the only kind that can never be repaid. They can only be cancelled.” She pushed off the wall. “We cannot go back to the flat. It is compromised.”

“Where, then?”

“The only place left,” she said, and there was a grim resignation in her voice. “The warehouse. The heart. If we are already in the hive, we might as well find the queen.”
  - The warehouse crouched in the northern industrial fringe, where the Seine turned sluggish and bore the weight of barges. It was a cathedral of decay, a long, low building of corrugated iron and soot-blackened brick. The windows were cataracts of grime. The light here did not lie; it was a thin, greasy smear from distant security lamps, making the pools of stagnant water in the broken asphalt shine like spilt oil.

This was the dangerous place. Not a villa with a marble hall, but a gutted shell where things were taken apart and put back together in new, terrible configurations. The dossier had been vague on its exact purpose. A transfer point. A holding station. The penultimate stop before the merchandise vanished into the stream of legitimate commerce.

Léa’s contact, the one who had given her the override codes for the villa, had given her this too: a hand-drawn map of the interior, and a time. “The night guard makes his round at 00:15. He is old, slow, loyal only to the bottle of cognac in his booth. You have twelve minutes between his passes. The inner cage is on a separate motion system. I do not have that code. That, you must find.”

We watched from the skeleton of a dead forklift. The cold here was industrial, metallic, leaching the heat from your core. It was the cold of a tool left out in the rain. At 00:14, a stooped figure in a too-large coat emerged from a side door, played a flashlight beam over a padlock, and shuffled back inside. The clock started.

“Traps?” I whispered.

“The man was not poetic. He said ‘dogs and wires.’ Assume both.”

We went in through a loading door whose rusted latch yielded to a sharp, upward blow from Léa’s multitool. The darkness inside was total, a solid thing. We clicked on our penlights, the beams cutting narrow, guilty tunnels through the dust. The air smelled of damp concrete, machine oil, and something else, something sweet and coppery and wrong.

The warehouse floor was a graveyard of forgotten industry: lathes under tarps, piles of pallets, stacks of yellowing fibreglass insulation. Our footsteps were muffled by the thick dust. Léa led, her light tracing a path along the wall, looking for the marks her contact had described—a faint blue chalk arrow pointing toward the office mezzanine.

A low growl vibrated in the darkness to our left.

We froze. The beam of my light found two points of greenish reflection, low to the ground. Then another set. Dogs. Not strays. These were thick-chested, short-haired, their muzzles striped with scars. They did not bark. They fanned out, silent, professional. Their breath made plumes in the cold air.

Léa reached slowly into her bag. She pulled out a small, wax-paper packet, tore it open with her teeth. The smell of raw, greasy meat filled the air. She tossed it, a underhand lob, into the deep shadows behind the dogs. The meat hit the concrete with a wet slap.

The dogs hesitated. One turned its head, nostrils flaring. The discipline warred with a deeper hunger. The largest, a brindle with a chewed ear, took a step toward the sound. Then it broke, and the others followed, a silent scramble into the dark. We heard the sounds of snarling and tearing.

“Bribed with better meat,” Léa murmured, already moving. “The oldest trick. It only works once.”

We found the stairs to the mezzanine—a rickety iron staircase that sang a note of protest with every step. The office was a glass-walled box overlooking the floor. The door was locked. A keypad blinked a steady red eye beside it. The inner cage.

Léa studied it. “Not a standard model. A hybrid. Motion sensor and code. If we trip the motion, it will likely seal the warehouse doors and flood the place with light.” She looked at me. “Your turn. The contact said the code would be here. Find it.”

I played my light over the desk inside, visible through the grimy glass. A telephone. A ledger. A coffee mug stained brown. A calendar, two years out of date. A photograph. I leaned closer. It was a family portrait, a man and a woman and two children on a beach. The man was the night guard. The photograph was tucked into the frame of the calendar. Something was written on the pale beach sand in front of the children’s feet. Numbers. Scratched there with a stick.

0704. A birthday. The oldest code in the world.

I read it out. Léa punched the numbers in. The red light blinked green. The lock gave a solid clunk.

Inside, the office was a tomb of paper and stale tobacco. But on the desk, next to the ledger, was a modern tablet, sleek and out of place. Léa woke it. It asked for a password. She tried the numbers. 0704. The screen unlocked.

She scrolled, her face illuminated by the cold blue glow. Her lips tightened. “Manifests. Bills of lading. End-user certificates. Forged, most of them. Syria. Libya. Yemen. A shopping list for the apocalypse.” She plugged a small drive into the port, her fingers flying. “Downloading. This is the proof. The chain.”

My light swept the room, looking for the rest of it—the thing we had come for, the meaning behind the pins and the photographs. In the corner, half-hidden by a filing cabinet, was a small, modern safe, gunmetal grey. No dial. A digital keypad and a card reader.

“Léa.”

She looked up, saw it. “That is not in the schematic. That is new.” She came over, studied it. “Military grade. We cannot open it. Not without the card and the code. And time.”

I stared at the safe. It hummed, a low, patient sound. This was the heart. Not the tablets, not the ledgers. Those were the circulatory system. This was the organ. Ibrahim’s scarab, Mercier’s weary calculations, Claude’s betrayal—they all led here, to this humming box in a frozen warehouse. My greatest fear was not death in this place. It was that this box would remain shut. That I would come all this way, through exile and lies and the unravelling of my past, and I would be faced with a final, blank door. A symbolic death. The death of knowing.

“We have to open it.”

“We cannot.”

“We have to.” My voice was quiet. I placed my bleeding hand against the cold metal. It was the only warmth in the room. “It’s why I’m here. It’s not the ring. It’s not the shipment. It’s what’s in here. The truth of what he was. What I loved. The final price of the market.”

Léa looked at me, and for a moment, I saw not the operative, but the woman who had also lost things to the great, grinding machine. She gave a curt nod. “Four minutes left. The guard. If he finds the dogs distracted, he may come early.” She returned to the tablet, downloading furiously.

I knelt before the safe. The keypad was blank. I thought of Mercier. A retired professor. A man of habits. I thought of the villa, the order, the specific brand of cognac on his sideboard. I thought of the photograph in his pocket. Not sentiment. A receipt.

I typed in the date from the photograph’s edge, the one in the box. The date stamped on the back of my memory: the day on the Corniche. 180591.

Red light.

I tried Ibrahim’s birthday.

Red light.

I tried the date of his death. The date the market corrected.

Red light.

The hum of the safe seemed to grow louder, a taunt. Outside, in the vast dark of the warehouse, one of the dogs let out a short, sharp yelp, then was silent. The fight over the meat was done. They would be coming back.

Think. He is a man who sees everything as a transaction. A calculation. What is the one number that matters?

And then I knew. It wasn’t a date of sentiment. It was a figure. A price.

I typed in the number from Claude’s original offer to me. The fee for finding a ring. The value assigned to my ignorance. 75000.

Green light.

A soft hiss as the locking bolts retracted.

I pulled the handle. The door swung open, soundless on its hinges.

Inside, there were no weapons. No stacks of currency. There were files. Neat, colour-coded dossiers. And a small, black jewellery box. I opened it.

Not a scarab. A ring. A heavy signet ring, gold. The crest was not a family emblem. It was a tiny, intricate scarab, surrounded by a stylised sun. The master ring. The one the driver had worn. The one in the photograph of Mercier and the general.

Beneath it, a single sheet of vellum. A list. Names. Codenames. Next to each, a sum of money, a date, and a status. Active. Terminated. Retired.
I found his name. Ibrahim al-Masri. Codename: SCRIBE. Recruited: 09/1991. Status: TERMINATED. 04/1999. Payout to beneficiary: 0. Notes: Asset developed moral reservations. Attempted to disengage. Liability.

Beneficiary: 0.

He had died for nothing. For less than nothing. For a zero in a ledger.

But it was the last name on the list that stopped my heart. The most recent entry. The ink was darker, the status still ACTIVE.

The name was my own.

Codename: RELIC.

Recruited: 10/2023. (Posthumous asset activation. Leverage: unresolved grief, financial precarity, proven investigative tenacity. Handler: CLAUDE.)

Status: ACTIVE.

Payout: 75000 (upon successful retrieval/destruction of this list).

Notes: Unaware. Operational deniability total. Useful for penetrating Mercier network due to genuine emotional drive. Recommend termination post-mission to close loop.

The world did not tilt. It ceased to exist. There was only the vellum, the cold blue light from the tablet, and the perfect, absolute silence of the vacuum where my self had been.

I was not a hunter. I was the bait. I was not a seeker of truth. I was the instrument of its burial. Claude had not hired me despite my connection to Ibrahim. He had hired me because of it. He had read my exile, my grief, my dwindling bank account, and he had seen a key. A one-time, disposable key to turn in a lock. My war was not mine. It was a script written for me in a ledger. My love, my loss, my very presence here in this frozen hell—all of it was a line item. A calculation.

The symbolic death was not the fear of the unopened safe. It was this. The opening. The sight of my own soul, itemised and priced. I had crossed the diaspora not to find answers, but to erase them. I was the ghost Mercier had spoken of, given weight and purpose and a target.

I heard the crunch of a footstep on gravel outside, far below. The guard. Early.

Léa looked up from the tablet. “Got it. We go.” She saw my face. “What is it?”

I could not speak. I handed her the vellum. She scanned it, her eyes widening. For a moment, the professional mask shattered, revealing pure, unadulterated horror. And pity. “Oh, mon Dieu.”

She understood instantly. The entire operation—her role, my role—was a set-up. Claude was not cleaning up a mess. He was cleaning house. We were both meant to find this, and we were both meant to die here, leaving the list destroyed and the network secure. I was the unwitting brush to apply the whitewash.

“We have to go. Now.” She shoved the tablet and the drive into her bag. She took the vellum from my numb fingers, folded it, put it in her own pocket. “This is the only copy. It is our life insurance.”

“He knows I’ve seen it,” I said, my voice a stranger’s. “Claude. He’ll know.”

“Then we must make sure he knows we have it. It is the only thing that will stay his hand.” She grabbed my arm, pulled me up. “Move.”

We fled the office, down the shrieking stairs. As we hit the main floor, a flashlight beam speared the darkness from the far end of the warehouse. The guard. “Qui est là?” His voice was old, querulous.

A shot rang out. Not from the guard. From above us, from the mezzanine railing. A suppressor’s wet thwip. The guard’s flashlight clattered to the ground, its beam spinning crazily. He cried out, a sound of shock and pain.

Claude. He was here. He had followed. Or he had never left.

“To the loading door!” Léa shoved me forward.

We ran, weaving through the labyrinth of machinery. Another shot. A spark erupted off a lathe to my right. He was herding us. We burst through the loading door into the bitter night, just as a car, sleek and black, slid to a halt in the yard, blocking our path to the alley. The passenger window descended. Mercier’s winter-eyed face looked out. He held a small, elegant pistol, resting it on the door.

“The list, mademoiselle,” he called, his voice almost bored. “And the drive. You have brought it to me. How efficient.”

We were pinned. Claude behind us in the warehouse. Mercier before us in the car. The aftermath of the ordeal was not relief. It was a new, tighter cage. We were vulnerable, exposed, and the enemy was not just aware—he was presented in two faces, both of which wanted us erased for different, converging reasons.

Léa stepped in front of me, slowly raising her hands. “The list is safe, Alain. It is not here. It is in a dead drop. If we do not check in by dawn, it goes to three newspapers and Interpol.”

Mercier sighed. “The classic bluff. Claude?”

From the warehouse doorway, Claude emerged. His pistol was pointed at the ground. His face was a mask of cold fury. “She is lying. She had no time. She has it on her.”

“There is one way to be sure,” Mercier said. He raised his pistol, aiming not at Léa, but at me. “You are a redundant copy, mademoiselle. A sentimental echo. The market has no use for you. But perhaps your death has some transactional value. It might loosen your friend’s tongue.”

He was right. I was a relic. An unwitting asset. A ghost with a price tag. The only thing of value I had left was my death. I looked from Mercier’s calm, calculating face to Claude’s furious one. I was the thread that connected them, the flaw in both their plans. The exile’s final, bitter revelation: you do not bring your war with you. You are the war. You are the territory being fought over by indifferent powers.

I took a step forward, out from behind Léa. I met Mercier’s winter gaze. I did not speak to him. I spoke to Claude.

“You recruited me,” I said, my voice carrying in the frozen air. “Codename: RELIC. Payout upon destruction of the list. Did you get a bonus for the termination fee, too?”

Claude’s mask slipped. Just for a second. Shock, then a furious confirmation. He had not known I knew the codename. That was the final proof.

Mercier’s finger tightened on the trigger. “Fascinating. Internal discord. The product turning on the handler. The market abhors inefficiency.”

In that moment, with the pistol aimed at my heart and the truth a cold stone in my gut, I did not think of Ibrahim. I did not think of the lake, or the café, or the lying light. I thought of the zero in the ledger next to his name. I would not be a zero. I would be an error. A crashing, catastrophic error in their perfect, silent calculation.

I smiled. It felt like a crack in ice.

“The list isn’t in a dead drop, Alain,” I said. “I memorised it. Every name. Every code. Every payout. You can kill me. But the ghost you weighted? It’s already speaking. And it knows your account number.”

For the first time, the winter in Mercier’s eyes flickered. With something like doubt. The sly, insinuating cold met something it could not quantify: a truth that could not be bought, only carried. Carried across water, across years, into the heart of the hive.

The stand-off held, a fragile, silent triangle in the greasy light. The guard moaned softly inside the warehouse. Somewhere on the river, a barge sounded its horn, a long, low note of mourning.

Then, from the mouth of the alley, came new headlights. Not one car. Two. They did not slow. They accelerated, engines roaring, tearing into the yard and skidding to a halt, doors flying open before they’d fully stopped.

Men emerged. Not Claude’s men. Not Mercier’s. They moved with a different kind of purpose, clad in dark tactical gear, their faces obscured. They raised compact assault rifles, the laser sights painting red dots on Claude, on Mercier’s car, on us.

A voice, amplified and metallic, shattered the night. “Police! Haut les mains! Drop your weapons!”

The cavalry had arrived. But it was not salvation. It was a new player. Another variable. As Claude and Mercier turned, stunned, toward this fresh threat, Léa grabbed my arm, her nails digging in.

“That,” she whispered, her voice thick with a dread deeper than any we had faced yet, “is not the police.”

The men advanced, their formation tight, professional, utterly silent save for the crunch of their boots on gravel. The red dots settled. One found my forehead. The light was a pinprick of pure, promise-less heat. It did not lie. It said only one thing: this will kill you.

And in that final, frozen second before the chaos erupted, I understood the hook upon which we were now caught. We had survived the heart of the dangerous place. We had faced the symbolic death and emerged, not with reward, but with a poison truth. We had left the enemy aware and enraged.

But we had also attracted something else. Something that had been waiting in the wings for the hive to be disturbed. The other war. The one that was not about markets or ledgers, but about something older, and darker, and far more personal.

The headlights from the new cars illuminated the face of the man leading the tactical team as he stepped forward. He pulled off his balaclava.

He was older. His hair was grey. But the set of his jaw, the dark, burning eyes—they were from the photograph. From the beach. From a life before the zero.

It was the beneficiary.

Ibrahim’s brother.



---

Chapter 6

CHAPTER 6

The light here is a liar. It is not the honeyed lie of a Paris café, nor the cleaver-honest glare off the lake. This is a surgical light, white and pitiless, spilled from the halogen eyes of the two black vans. It carves the scene into stark planes: the crushed gravel, the shocked faces of Claude and Mercier frozen in its beam, the glint of a dropped pistol, the dark, wet earth where a man had fallen earlier and would not rise. It turns the night into a specimen, pinned and quivering. And in the center of this false, sterile day stands the beneficiary. Ibrahim’s brother. His name, I would learn, was Kader.

He did not look at the weapons trained on him by Claude’s men, or at Mercier’s driver still crouched behind an open door. He looked only at me. His gaze was not the burning coal of the photograph; time had banked that fire into something harder, a flinty obsidian that absorbed the light and gave nothing back. It was the look of a man who has turned a ledger over and over in his mind for twenty years, memorizing the weight of every debt.

The amplified voice had ceased. The silence it left was a vacuum, into which the small sounds rushed: Léa’s shallow breath beside me, the creak of a boot from one of the tactical team, the distant, indifferent hum of the autoroute. The red dot on my forehead was a third eye, seeing only the end of things.

Kader took another step forward. He moved like his brother had—a economical grace, a sense of contained force. But where Ibrahim’s energy had been kinetic, ready to spill into a story or a gesture, Kader’s was potential, coiled and specific.

“You,” he said. His voice was low, unamplified, yet it carried through the frozen tableau. It was accented, but not like Ibrahim’s.
